	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Jewish Christian</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">

		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the historical concept. For a comparison of the religions as they exist today, see <a href="/wiki/Christianity_and_Judaism" title="Christianity and Judaism">Christianity and Judaism</a>. For the modern-day religious movement, see <a href="/wiki/Messianic_Judaism" title="Messianic Judaism">Messianic Judaism</a>.</div>

<ul><li><a href="/wiki/File:Star_of_David.svg" class="image"><img alt="Star of David.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_of_David.svg/14px-Star_of_David.svg.png" decoding="async" width="14" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_of_David.svg/21px-Star_of_David.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_of_David.svg/28px-Star_of_David.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="260" data-file-height="300" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Judaism" title="Portal:Judaism">Judaism&#32;portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Judaism" title="Template:Judaism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Judaism" title="Template talk:Judaism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Judaism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">Early Christianity</a> had its roots in <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Judaism" title="Hellenistic Judaism">Hellenistic Judaism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Messiah_in_Judaism" title="Messiah in Judaism">Jewish messianism</a> of the first century and <b>Jewish Christians</b> were the first <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a>. Christianity started with <a href="/wiki/Jewish_eschatology" title="Jewish eschatology">Jewish eschatological</a> expectations, and it developed into the veneration of a deified <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> after his earthly <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Jesus" title="Ministry of Jesus">ministry</a>, his <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_of_Jesus" title="Crucifixion of Jesus">crucifixion</a>, and the post-crucifixion experiences of his followers.
</p><p>The inclusion of <a href="/wiki/Gentile" title="Gentile">gentiles</a> led to a growing split between Jewish Christians and gentile Christianity. From the latter <a href="/wiki/Great_Church" title="Great Church">"orthodox" Christianity</a> eventually arose, while mainstream Judaism developed into <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_Judaism" title="Rabbinic Judaism">Rabbinic Judaism</a>. Jewish Christians drifted apart from mainstream Judaism, eventually becoming a minority strand which had mostly disappeared by the fifth century.
</p><p>The split of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> took place during the <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">first centuries CE</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Shiffman_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shiffman-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JVL_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JVL-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> While the <a href="/wiki/First_Jewish%E2%80%93Roman_War" title="First Jewish–Roman War">First Jewish–Roman War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(70_CE)" title="Siege of Jerusalem (70 CE)">destruction of the Second Temple</a> in 70 CE were main events, the separation was a long-term process, in which the boundaries were not clear-cut.<sup id="cite_ref-Shiffman_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shiffman-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JVL_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JVL-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Early Jewish Christians referred to themselves as 'The Way' (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">ἡ ὁδός</span> - hė odós), probably coming from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblica.com/bible/?osis=niv:Isaiah.40:3–40:3">Isaiah 40:3</a>, "prepare the way of the Lord."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECwiekowski198879-80_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECwiekowski198879-80-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPao201665_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPao201665-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> According to <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblica.com/bible/?osis=niv:Acts.11:26–11:26">Acts 11:26</a>, the term "Christian" (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">Χριστιανός</span>) was first used in reference to Jesus's <a href="/wiki/Disciple_(Christianity)" title="Disciple (Christianity)">disciples</a> in the city of <a href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Antioch" title="Early centers of Christianity">Antioch</a>, meaning "followers of Christ," by the non-Jewish inhabitants of Antioch.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The earliest recorded use of the term "Christianity" (Greek: <span lang="el">Χριστιανισμός</span>) was by <a href="/wiki/Ignatius_of_Antioch" title="Ignatius of Antioch">Ignatius of Antioch</a>, in around 100&#160;AD.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElwellComfort2001266,_828_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElwellComfort2001266,_828-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "Jewish Christian" appears in historical texts contrasting Christians of <a href="/wiki/Jewish" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish">Jewish</a> origin with <a href="/wiki/Gentile" title="Gentile">gentile</a> Christians, both in discussion of the <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Apostolic Age">New Testament church</a><sup id="cite_ref-Judaeo-Christians_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Judaeo-Christians-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shiffman_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shiffman-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JVL_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JVL-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tabor_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tabor-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Ante-Nicene_Period" class="mw-redirect" title="Ante-Nicene Period">second and following centuries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> It is also a term used for Jews who converted to Christianity but kept their Jewish heritage and traditions.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple_Period" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Temple Period">Second Temple Period</a>, <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_Judaism" title="Origins of Judaism">Origins of Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Judaism" title="Hellenistic Judaism">Hellenistic Judaism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_1st_century" title="Christianity in the 1st century">Christianity in the 1st century</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewish-Hellenistic_background">Jewish-Hellenistic background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Jewish-Hellenistic background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenism">Hellenism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Hellenism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple_Judaism" title="Second Temple Judaism">Second Temple Judaism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Judaism" title="Hellenistic Judaism">Hellenistic Judaism</a></div>
<p>Christianity arose in the syncretistic Hellenistic world of the first century CE, which was dominated by Roman law and Greek culture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMack1995_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMack1995-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenistic culture">Hellenistic culture</a> had a profound impact on the customs and practices of Jews, both in the <a href="/wiki/Land_of_Israel" title="Land of Israel">Land of Israel</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_diaspora" title="Jewish diaspora">Diaspora</a>. The inroads into Judaism gave rise to Hellenistic Judaism in the Jewish diaspora which sought to establish a <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Hebraic-Jewish religious tradition</a> within the culture and language of <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenism</a>.
</p><p>Hellenistic Judaism spread to <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Ptolemaic Egypt">Ptolemaic Egypt</a> from the 3rd century BCE, and became a notable <i><a href="/wiki/Religio_licita" title="Religio licita">religio licita</a></i> after the <a href="/wiki/Roman_conquest_of_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman conquest of Greece">Roman conquest of Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asia_(Roman_province)" title="Asia (Roman province)">Anatolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Syria" title="Roman Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Judea" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Judea">Judea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Egypt">Egypt</a>, until its decline in the 3rd century parallel to the rise of <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">Gnosticism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">Early Christianity</a>.
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Burton_Mack" class="mw-redirect" title="Burton Mack">Burton Mack</a>, the Christian vision of Jesus' death for the redemption of mankind was only possible in a Hellenised milieu.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewish_sects">Jewish sects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Jewish sects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>During the early first century CE there were many competing Jewish sects in the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a>, and those that became <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_Judaism" title="Rabbinic Judaism">Rabbinic Judaism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Proto-orthodox_Christianity" title="Proto-orthodox Christianity">Proto-orthodox Christianity</a> were but two of these. There were <a href="/wiki/Pharisees" title="Pharisees">Pharisees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sadducees" title="Sadducees">Sadducees</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zealots_(Judea)" class="mw-redirect" title="Zealots (Judea)">Zealots</a>, but also other less influential sects, including the <a href="/wiki/Essenes" title="Essenes">Essenes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Shiffman_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shiffman-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JVL_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JVL-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The first century BCE and first century CE saw a growing number of charismatic religious leaders contributing to what would become the <a href="/wiki/Mishnah" title="Mishnah">Mishnah</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_Judaism" title="Rabbinic Judaism">Rabbinic Judaism</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Jesus" title="Ministry of Jesus">ministry of Jesus</a>, which would lead to the emergence of the first Jewish Christian community.<sup id="cite_ref-Shiffman_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shiffman-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JVL_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JVL-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the gospels contain strong condemnations of the Pharisees, <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul the Apostle</a> claimed to be a Pharisee, and there is a clear influence of <a href="/wiki/Hillel_the_Elder" title="Hillel the Elder">Hillel</a>'s interpretation of the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a> in the Gospel-sayings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeman2015145-146_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeman2015145-146-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Belief in the resurrection of the dead in the <a href="/wiki/Messianic_age" class="mw-redirect" title="Messianic age">messianic age</a> was a core Pharisaic doctrine.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewish_and_Christian_messianism">Jewish and Christian messianism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Jewish and Christian messianism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Jewish_eschatology" title="Jewish eschatology">Jewish eschatology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Messiah_in_Judaism" title="Messiah in Judaism">Messiah in Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Messiah_ben_Joseph" title="Messiah ben Joseph">Messiah ben Joseph</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rejection_of_Jesus" title="Rejection of Jesus">Rejection of Jesus</a></div>
<p>Most of Jesus's teachings were intelligible and acceptable in terms of Second Temple Judaism; what set Christians apart from Jews was their faith in Christ as the resurrected messiah.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen1987167–168_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen1987167–168-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> While Christianity acknowledges only one ultimate Messiah, Judaism can be said to hold to a concept of multiple messiahs. The two most relevant are the Messiah ben Joseph and the traditional Messiah ben David. Some scholars have argued that the idea of two messiahs, one suffering and the second fulfilling the traditional messianic role, was normative to ancient Judaism, predating Jesus. Jesus would have been viewed by many as one or both.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jewish_messianism" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish messianism">Jewish messianism</a> has its root in the <a href="/wiki/Apocalyptic_literature" title="Apocalyptic literature">apocalyptic literature</a> of the 2nd century BCE to the 1st century CE, promising a future "anointed" leader or <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">Messiah</a> to resurrect the Israelite "<a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_God" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of God">Kingdom of God</a>", in place of the foreign rulers of the time. According to <a href="/wiki/Shaye_J.D._Cohen" class="mw-redirect" title="Shaye J.D. Cohen">Shaye J.D. Cohen</a>, Jesus's failure to establish an independent Israel, and his death at the hands of the Romans, caused many Jews to reject him as the Messiah.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen1987168_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen1987168-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;note 3&#93;</a></sup> Jews at that time were expecting a military leader as a Messiah, such as <a href="/wiki/Bar_Kokhba" class="mw-redirect" title="Bar Kokhba">Bar Kokhba</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jesus">Jesus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Jesus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="box-Off_topic plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="40" data-file-height="40" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>may <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Guide_to_writing_better_articles#Stay_on_topic" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Guide to writing better articles">stray from the topic</a> of the article</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> or discuss this issue on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Jewish_Christian" title="Talk:Jewish Christian">talk page</a>.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2019</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Historical_background_of_the_New_Testament" title="Historical background of the New Testament">Historical background of the New Testament</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_views">Christian views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Christian views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Life_of_Jesus_in_the_New_Testament" title="Life of Jesus in the New Testament">Life of Jesus in the New Testament</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Jesus" title="Ministry of Jesus">Ministry of Jesus</a></div>
<p>According to Christian denominations the bodily <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">resurrection of Jesus</a> after his death is the pivotal event of Jesus' life and death, as described in the gospels and the epistles. According to the gospels, written decades after the events of his life, Jesus preached for a period of one to three years in the early 1st century. His ministry of teaching, healing the sick and disabled and performing <a href="/wiki/Miracles_of_Jesus" title="Miracles of Jesus">various miracles</a> culminated in his <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_of_Jesus" title="Crucifixion of Jesus">crucifixion</a> at the hands of the Roman authorities in Jerusalem. After his death, he appeared to his followers, <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">resurrected</a> from death. After forty days he ascended to Heaven, but his followers believed he would <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">soon return</a> to usher in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_God" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of God">Kingdom of God</a> and fulfill the rest of <a href="/wiki/Messianic_prophecy" class="mw-redirect" title="Messianic prophecy">Messianic prophecy</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_dead" title="Resurrection of the dead">resurrection of the dead</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">Last Judgment</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholarly_views">Scholarly views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Scholarly views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Historical_Jesus" title="Historical Jesus">Historical Jesus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quest_for_the_historical_Jesus" title="Quest for the historical Jesus">Quest for the historical Jesus</a></div>
<p>Proponents of higher criticism claim that regardless of how one interprets the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Jesus" title="Ministry of Jesus">mission of Jesus</a>, he must be understood in context as a 1st-century Middle Eastern Jew.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is widespread disagreement among scholars on the details of the life of Jesus mentioned in the gospel narratives, and on the meaning of his teachings.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Scholars often draw a distinction between the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Jesus" title="Historical Jesus">Jesus of history</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christ of faith</a>, and two different accounts can be found in this regard.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Critical scholarship has stripped away most narratives about Jesus as <a href="/wiki/Legend" title="Legend">legendary</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Historical_Jesus" title="Historical Jesus">mainstream historical view</a> is that while the gospels include many legendary elements, these are religious elaborations added to the accounts of a historical Jesus who was crucified under the Roman prefect <a href="/wiki/Pontius_Pilate" title="Pontius Pilate">Pontius Pilate</a> in the 1st-century Roman province of <a href="/wiki/Judea_(Roman_province)" title="Judea (Roman province)">Judea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman2012_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman2012-32">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStanton2002143ff_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStanton2002143ff-33">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> His remaining disciples later believed that he was resurrected.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorter1999_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorter1999-34">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ehrman.Triumph_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrman.Triumph-35">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Five <a href="/wiki/Portraits_of_the_historical_Jesus" class="mw-redirect" title="Portraits of the historical Jesus">portraits of the historical Jesus</a> are supported by mainstream scholars, namely the <a href="/wiki/Apocalypticism" title="Apocalypticism">apocalyptic</a> <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophet</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;note 4&#93;</a></sup> the charismatic healer,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Cynicism_(philosophy)" title="Cynicism (philosophy)">Cynic</a> philosopher, the Jewish Messiah, and the prophet of social change.<sup id="cite_ref-Cradel124_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cradel124-41">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CambHist23_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CambHist23-42">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Jewish_Christianity">Early Jewish Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Early Jewish Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Most historians agree that Jesus or his followers established a new <a href="/wiki/Jewish_religious_movements" title="Jewish religious movements">Jewish sect</a>, one that attracted both Jewish and gentile converts. According to New Testament scholar <a href="/wiki/Bart_D._Ehrman" title="Bart D. Ehrman">Bart D. Ehrman</a>, a number of early Christianities existed in the first century CE, from which developed various Christian traditions and denominations, including <a href="/wiki/Proto-orthodox_Christianity" title="Proto-orthodox Christianity">proto-orthodoxy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman2005_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman2005-43">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Theologian" class="mw-redirect" title="Theologian">theologian</a> <a href="/wiki/James_Dunn_(theologian)" title="James Dunn (theologian)">James D. G. Dunn</a>, four types of early Christianity can be discerned: Jewish Christianity, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Hellenistic Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apocalypticism" title="Apocalypticism">Apocalyptic Christianity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="History of the Catholic Church">early Catholicism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2006253-255_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2006253-255-44">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first followers of Jesus were essentially all ethnically Jewish or Jewish <a href="/wiki/Proselytes" class="mw-redirect" title="Proselytes">proselytes</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cultural_and_historical_background_of_Jesus" class="mw-redirect" title="Cultural and historical background of Jesus">Jesus was Jewish</a>, preached to the Jewish people, and called from them his first followers. According to McGrath, <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Christians" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish Christians">Jewish Christians</a>, as faithful religious Jews, "regarded their movement as an affirmation of every aspect of contemporary Judaism, with the addition of one extra belief-that Jesus was the Messiah."<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Jewish Christians were the original members of the <a href="/wiki/Split_of_early_Christianity_and_Judaism" class="mw-redirect" title="Split of early Christianity and Judaism">Jewish movement</a> that later became <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Judaeo-Christians_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Judaeo-Christians-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Freedman_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Freedman-46">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shiffman_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shiffman-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JVL_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JVL-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In the earliest stage the community was made up of all those Jews who believed that Jesus was the <a href="/wiki/Messiah_in_Judaism" title="Messiah in Judaism">Jewish messiah</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Shiffman_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shiffman-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JVL_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JVL-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> As Christianity grew and developed, Jewish Christians became only one strand of the <a href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity" title="Early centers of Christianity">early Christian community</a>, characterised by combining the <a href="/wiki/Confession_of_Peter" title="Confession of Peter">confession of Jesus as Christ</a> with continued <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">observance of the Torah</a><sup id="cite_ref-Judaeo-Christians_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Judaeo-Christians-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and adherence to Jewish traditions such as <a href="/wiki/Shabbat" title="Shabbat">Sabbath observance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jewish_calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish calendar">Jewish calendar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Halakhah" class="mw-redirect" title="Halakhah">Jewish laws and customs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brit_milah" title="Brit milah">circumcision</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kosher" class="mw-redirect" title="Kosher">kosher diet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Synagogue" title="Synagogue">synagogue</a> attendance, and by a direct genetic relationship to the earliest followers of Jesus.<sup id="cite_ref-Judaeo-Christians_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Judaeo-Christians-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Freedman_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Freedman-46">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shiffman_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shiffman-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tabor_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tabor-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Jerusalem_ekkl.C4.93sia"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Jerusalem_ekklēsia">Jerusalem <i>ekklēsia</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Jerusalem ekklēsia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Flight_to_Pella" title="Flight to Pella">Flight to Pella</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Jerusalem" title="Early centers of Christianity">Jerusalem Church</a> was an early Christian community located in Jerusalem, of which <a href="/wiki/James_the_Just" class="mw-redirect" title="James the Just">James the Just</a>, the brother of Jesus, and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">Peter</a> were leaders. <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul</a> was in contact with this community.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Legitimised by <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">Jesus' appearance</a>, Peter was the first leader of the Jerusalem <i>ekklēsia</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPagels200545_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPagels200545-49">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELüdemannÖzen1996116_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELüdemannÖzen1996116-50">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> He was soon eclipsed in this leadership by James the Just, "the Brother of the Lord,"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPagels200545-46_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPagels200545-46-51">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELüdemannÖzen1996116-117_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELüdemannÖzen1996116-117-52">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> which may explain why the early texts contain scarce information about Peter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELüdemannÖzen1996116-117_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELüdemannÖzen1996116-117-52">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> According to Lüdemann, in the discussions about the <a href="/wiki/Paul_and_Judaism" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul and Judaism">strictness of adherence</a> to the Jewish Law, the more conservative faction of James the Just took the overhand over the more liberal position of Peter, who soon lost influence.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELüdemannÖzen1996116-117_52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELüdemannÖzen1996116-117-52">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> According to Dunn, this was not an "usurpation of power," but a consequence of Peter's involvement in missionary activities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBockmuehl201052_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBockmuehl201052-53">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">Eusebius</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Church_History_(Eusebius)" title="Church History (Eusebius)">Church History</a></i> 4.5.3–4: the first 15 Christian <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Patriarch_of_Jerusalem#Bishops_of_Jerusalem" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthodox Patriarch of Jerusalem">Bishops of Jerusalem</a> were "of the circumcision". The Romans destroyed the Jewish leadership in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem_in_Christianity" title="Jerusalem in Christianity">Jerusalem</a> in year 135 during the <a href="/wiki/Bar_Kokhba_revolt" title="Bar Kokhba revolt">Bar Kokhba revolt</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> but it is traditionally believed the Jerusalem Christians waited out the <a href="/wiki/Jewish%E2%80%93Roman_wars" title="Jewish–Roman wars">Jewish–Roman wars</a> in <a href="/wiki/Flight_to_Pella" title="Flight to Pella">Pella</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Decapolis" title="Decapolis">Decapolis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs">Beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Pauline_epistles" title="Pauline epistles">Pauline epistles</a> incorporate <a href="/wiki/Creed" title="Creed">creeds</a>, or confessions of faith, of a belief in an exalted Christ that predate Paul,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMack1995_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMack1995-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> and give essential information on the faith of the early Jerusalem Church around James, 'the brother of Jesus'.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> This group venerated the risen Christ, who had appeared to several persons,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMack1995_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMack1995-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> as in <a href="/wiki/Philippians_2" title="Philippians 2">Philippians 2</a>:6–11, the Christ hymn, which portrays Jesus as an incarnated and subsequently exalted heavenly being.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-59">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Messiah.2FChrist"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Messiah/Christ">Messiah/Christ</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Messiah/Christ">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Messiah_in_Judaism" title="Messiah in Judaism">Messiah in Judaism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eschatology" title="Eschatology">Eschatology</a></div>
<p>Early Christians regarded Jesus to be the Messiah, the promised king who would restore the Jewish kingdom and independence. Jewish messianism has its root in the <a href="/wiki/Apocalyptic_literature" title="Apocalyptic literature">apocalyptic literature</a> of the 2nd century BCE to 1st century BCE, promising a future "anointed" leader or <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">messiah</a> to restore the Israelite "<a href="/wiki/Kingship_and_kingdom_of_God" title="Kingship and kingdom of God">Kingdom of God</a>", in place of the foreign rulers of the time. This corresponded with the <a href="/wiki/Maccabean_Revolt" title="Maccabean Revolt">Maccabean Revolt</a> directed against the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>. Following the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Hasmonean" class="mw-redirect" title="Hasmonean">Hasmonean</a> kingdom, it was directed against the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman</a> administration of <a href="/wiki/Judea_(Roman_province)" title="Judea (Roman province)">Judea Province</a>, which, according to <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a>, began with the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Zealots" title="Zealots">Zealots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sicarii" title="Sicarii">Sicarii</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Census_of_Quirinius" title="Census of Quirinius">Census of Quirinius</a> (6 CE), although full-scale open revolt did not occur until the <a href="/wiki/First_Jewish%E2%80%93Roman_War" title="First Jewish–Roman War">First Jewish–Roman War</a> in 66 CE.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Resurrection">Resurrection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Resurrection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to the New Testament, some Christians reported that they <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_appearances_of_Jesus" class="mw-redirect" title="Resurrection appearances of Jesus">encountered Jesus after his crucifixion</a>. They argued that he had been <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">resurrected</a> (belief in the resurrection of the dead in the <a href="/wiki/Messianic_Age" title="Messianic Age">Messianic Age</a> was a core <a href="/wiki/Pharisees" title="Pharisees">Pharisaic</a> doctrine), and would <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">soon return</a> to usher in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_God" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of God">Kingdom of God</a> and fulfill the rest of <a href="/wiki/Messianic_prophecy" class="mw-redirect" title="Messianic prophecy">Messianic prophecy</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_dead" title="Resurrection of the dead">resurrection of the dead</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">Last Judgment</a>.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Resurrection_experiences">Resurrection experiences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Resurrection experiences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p><a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(American_Standard)/1_Corinthians#15:3" class="extiw" title="s:Bible (American Standard)/1 Corinthians">1 Corinthians 15:3-9</a> gives an early testimony, which was delivered to Paul,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> of the atonement of Jesus and the appearances of the risen Christ to "Cephas and the twelve", and to "James [...] and all the apostles", possibly reflecting a fusion of two early Christian groups:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>3 For I delivered unto you first of all that which also I received: that Christ died for our sins according to the scriptures;<br />
</p><p>4 and that he was buried; and that he hath been raised on the third day according to the scriptures;<br />
5 and that he appeared to Cephas; then to the twelve;<br />
6 then he appeared to above five hundred brethren at once, of whom the greater part remain until now, but some are fallen asleep;<br />
7 then he appeared to James; then to all the apostles;<br />
8 and last of all, as to the [child] untimely born, he appeared to me also.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>According to Geza Vermes, the concept of resurrection formed "the initial stage of the belief in his exaltation", which is "the apogee of the triumphant Christ".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVermes2008138-139_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVermes2008138-139-62">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The focal concern of the early communities is the expected return of Jesus, and the entry of the believers into the kingdom of God with a transformed body.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVermes2008139_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVermes2008139-63">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Ehrman, the resurrection experiences were a denial response to his disciples' sudden disillusionment following Jesus' death. According to Ehrman, some of his followers claimed to have seen him alive again, resulting in a multitude of stories which convinced others that Jesus had risen from death and was exalted to Heaven.<sup id="cite_ref-Ehrman.1april2018_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrman.1april2018-37">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;note 5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Paula_Fredriksen" title="Paula Fredriksen">Paula Fredriksen</a>, Jesus's impact on his followers was so great that they could not accept the failure implicit in his death.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-65">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> According to Fredricksen, before his death Jesus created amongst his believers such certainty that the Kingdom of God and the resurrection of the dead was at hand, that with few exceptions (John 20: 24-29) when they saw him shortly after his execution, they had no doubt that he had been resurrected, and the general resurrection of the dead was at hand. These specific beliefs were compatible with Second Temple Judaism.<sup id="cite_ref-Paula_Fredricksen_pp._133-134_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paula_Fredricksen_pp._133-134-66">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Johan Leman, the resurrection must be understood as a sense of presence of Jesus even after his death, especially during the ritual meals which were continued after his death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeman2015167-183_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeman2015167-183-67">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> His early followers regarded him as a <a href="/wiki/Righteousness" title="Righteousness">righteous</a> man and prophet, who was therefore resurrected and exalted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeman2015173-174_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeman2015173-174-68">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> In time, Messianistic, <a href="/wiki/Isaiah" title="Isaiah">Isaiahic</a>, apocalyptic and eschatological expectations were blended in the experience and understanding of Jesus, who came to be expected to return to earth.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeman2015173-174_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeman2015173-174-68">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Bodily_resurrection">Bodily resurrection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Bodily resurrection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>A point of debate is how Christians came to believe in a bodily resurrection, which was "a comparatively recent development within Judaism."<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> According to Dag Øistein Endsjø, "The notion of the resurrection of the flesh was, as we have seen, not unknown to certain parts of Judaism in antiquity", but Paul rejected the idea of bodily resurrection, and it also can't be found within the strands of Jewish thought in which he was formed.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> According to Porter, Hayes and Tombs, the Jewish tradition emphasizes a continued spiritual existence rather than a bodily resurrection.<sup id="cite_ref-Porter.Hayes.Tombs_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porter.Hayes.Tombs-71">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nevertheless, the origin of this idea is commonly traced to Jewish beliefs,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> a view against which Stanley E. Porter objected.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorter1999_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorter1999-34">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> According to Porter, Jewish and subsequent Christian thought were influenced by Greek thoughts, were "assumptions regarding resurrection" can be found,<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> which were probably adopted by Paul.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;note 6&#93;</a></sup> According to Ehrman, most of the alleged parallels between Jesus and the pagan savior-gods only exist in the modern imagination, and there are no "accounts of others who were born to virgin mothers and who died as an atonement for sin and then were raised from the dead."<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Exaltation_and_deification">Exaltation and deification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Exaltation and deification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to Ehrman, a central question in the research on Jesus and early Christianity is how a human came to be deified in a relatively short time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman2014_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman2014-76">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Jewish Christians like the Ebionites had an <a href="/wiki/Adoptionist" class="mw-redirect" title="Adoptionist">Adoptionist</a> <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christology</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKloppenborg1994435–9_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKloppenborg1994435–9-77">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> and regarded Jesus as the Messiah while rejecting his <a href="/wiki/Divinity" title="Divinity">divinity</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Ebionites_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ebionites-78">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> while other strands of Christian thought regard Jesus to be a "fully divine figure", a "high Christology".<sup id="cite_ref-Bouma_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bouma-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> How soon the earthly Jesus was regarded to be the incarnation of God is a matter of scholarly debate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman2014_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman2014-76">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bouma_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bouma-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Philippians_2" title="Philippians 2">Philippians 2</a>:6–11 contains the Christ hymn, which portrays Jesus as an incarnated and subsequently exalted heavenly being:<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-59">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>5 Have this mind in you, which was also in Christ Jesus:<br />
</p><p>6 who, existing in the form of God, counted not the being on an equality with God a thing to be grasped,<br />
7 but emptied himself, taking the form of a servant, being made in the likeness of men;<br />
8 and being found in fashion as a man, he humbled himself, becoming obedient [even] unto death, yea, the death of the cross.<br />
9 Wherefore also God highly exalted him, and gave unto him the name which is above every name;<br />
10 that in the name of Jesus every knee should bow, of [things] in heaven and [things] on earth and [things] under the earth,<br />
11 and that every tongue should confess that Jesus Christ is Lord, to the glory of God the Father.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>According to Dunn, the background of this hymn has been strongly debated. Some see it as influenced by a Greek worldview.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;note 7&#93;</a></sup> while others have argued for Jewish influences. According to Dunn, the hymn contains a contrast with the sins of Adam and his disobedience. Dunn further notes that the hymn may be seen as a three-stage Christology, starting with "an earlier stage of mythic pre-history or pre-existence," but regards the humility-exaltation contrast to be the main theme.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2006146-147_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2006146-147-80">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This belief in the incarnated and exalted Christ was part of Christian tradition a few years after his death and over a decade before the writing of the Pauline epistles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman2014_76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman2014-76">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bouma_38-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bouma-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> According to Dunn, the background of this hymn has been strongly debated. Some see it as influenced by a Greek worldview,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;note 8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Burton_L._Mack" title="Burton L. Mack">Burton L. Mack</a> the early Christian communities started with "Jesus movements" new religious movements centering on a human teacher called Jesus. A number of these "Jesus movements" can be discerned in early Christian writings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMack1997_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMack1997-83">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> According to Mack, within these Jesus-movements developed within 25 years the belief that Jesus was the Messiah, and had risen from death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMack1995_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMack1995-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Erhman, the gospels show a development from a "low Christology" towards a "high Christology".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEhrman2014_76-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEhrman2014-76">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Yet, a "high Christology" seems to have been part of Christian traditions a few years after his death, and over a decade before the writing of the Pauline epistles, which are the oldest Christian writings.<sup id="cite_ref-Bouma_38-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bouma-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> According to Martin Hengel, as summarized by Jeremy Bouma, the letters of Paul already contain a fully developed Christology, shortly after the death of Jesus, including references to his pre-existence<sup id="cite_ref-Bouma_38-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bouma-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> According to Hengel, the Gospel of John shows a development which builds on this early high Christology, fusing it with <a href="/wiki/Wisdom_(personification)" title="Wisdom (personification)">Jewish wisdom traditions</a>, in which Wisdom was personified an descended into the world. While this "Logos Christology" is recognizable for Greek metaphysics, it is nevertheless not derived from pagan sources, and Hengel rejects the idea of influence from "Hellenistic mystery cults or a Gnostic redeemer myth".<sup id="cite_ref-Bouma_38-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bouma-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewish_practices_and_identity">Jewish practices and identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Jewish practices and identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Acts" class="mw-redirect" title="Book of Acts">Book of Acts</a> reports that the early followers continued daily <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple" title="Second Temple">Temple</a> attendance and traditional Jewish home prayer. Other passages in the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> gospels reflect a similar observance of traditional Jewish piety such as <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">fasting</a>, reverence for the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a> and observance of <a href="/wiki/Jewish_holiday" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish holiday">Jewish holy days</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Paul_and_the_inclusion_of_gentiles">Paul and the inclusion of gentiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Paul and the inclusion of gentiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg/200px-File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg/300px-File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg/400px-File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg 2x" data-file-width="599" data-file-height="445" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist depiction of <i>Saint Paul Writing His Epistles</i>, 16th century (Blaffer Foundation Collection, <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>)</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle_and_Judaism" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul the Apostle and Judaism">Paul the Apostle and Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_the_Old_Covenant" title="Christian views on the Old Covenant">Christian views on the Old Covenant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Incident_at_Antioch" title="Incident at Antioch">Incident at Antioch</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pauline_Christianity" title="Pauline Christianity">Pauline Christianity</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paul_of_Tarsus">Paul of Tarsus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Paul of Tarsus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to Larry Hurtado, "the christology and devotional stance that Paul affirmed (and shared with others in the early Jesus-movement) was [...] a distinctive expression within a variegated body of Jewish messianic hopes."<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;web 1&#93;</a></sup> According to Dunn, Paul presents, in his <a href="/wiki/Pauline_epistles" title="Pauline epistles">epistles</a>, a Hellenised Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2006_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2006-85">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;note 9&#93;</a></sup> According to Ehrman, "Paul's message, in a nutshell, was a Jewish apocalyptic proclamation with a seriously Christian twist."<sup id="cite_ref-Ehrman.Triumph_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrman.Triumph-35">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Paul was in contact with the early Christian community in <a href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Jerusalem" title="Early centers of Christianity">Jerusalem</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/James_the_Just" class="mw-redirect" title="James the Just">James the Just</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMack1997_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMack1997-83">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;note 10&#93;</a></sup> Fragments of their beliefs in an exalted and deified Jesus, what Mack called the "Christ cult," can be found in the writings of Paul.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMack1997_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMack1997-83">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;note 11&#93;</a></sup> According to the New Testament, Saul of Tarsus first persecuted the early Jewish Christians, but then <a href="/wiki/Conversion_of_Paul_the_Apostle" title="Conversion of Paul the Apostle">converted</a>. He adopted the name Paul and started <a href="/wiki/Proselytism" title="Proselytism">proselytizing</a> among the gentiles, adopting the title "Apostle to the Gentiles". He persuaded the leaders of the Jerusalem Church to allow gentile converts exemption from most <a href="/wiki/Mitzvot" class="mw-redirect" title="Mitzvot">Jewish commandments</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Jerusalem" title="Council of Jerusalem">Council of Jerusalem</a>, which opened the way for a much larger Christian Church, extending far beyond the Jewish community.
</p><p>While Paul was inspired by the early Christian apostles, his writings elaborate on their teachings, and also give interpretations which are different from other teachings as documented in the <a href="/wiki/Canonical_gospels" class="mw-redirect" title="Canonical gospels">canonical gospels</a>, early <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts</a> and the rest of the New Testament, such as the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_James" title="Epistle of James">Epistle of James</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMack_1995_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMack_1995-91">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaccoby1986_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaccoby1986-92">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Paul was, before his conversion, an antagonist of the followers of Jesus. Initially he persecuted the "church of God."<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;note 12&#93;</a></sup> Then <a href="/wiki/Conversion_of_Paul" class="mw-redirect" title="Conversion of Paul">converted</a>, starting to proselytize among the gentiles.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inclusion_of_gentiles">Inclusion of gentiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Inclusion of gentiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some early Jewish Christians believed that non-Jews must <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Judaism" title="Conversion to Judaism">convert to Judaism</a> and adopt <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">Jewish customs</a> in order to be saved. Paul criticized Peter for himself abandoning these customs, and therefore presenting a poor example to non-Jews joining the Christians.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Paul's close coworker <a href="/wiki/Barnabas" title="Barnabas">Barnabas</a> sided with Peter in this dispute.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Those that taught that gentile converts to Christianity ought to adopt more Jewish practices to be saved, however, were called "<a href="/wiki/Judaizers" title="Judaizers">Judaizers</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Damick2011_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Damick2011-101">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Though the <a href="/wiki/Apostle_Peter" class="mw-redirect" title="Apostle Peter">Apostle Peter</a> was initially sympathetic, the <a href="/wiki/Apostle_Paul" class="mw-redirect" title="Apostle Paul">Apostle Paul</a> opposed the teaching at the <a href="/wiki/Incident_at_Antioch" title="Incident at Antioch">Incident at Antioch</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Gal.+2%3A11-21&amp;version=NRSV">Gal. 2:11-21</a></span>) and at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Jerusalem" title="Council of Jerusalem">Council of Jerusalem</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Acts+15%3A6-35&amp;version=NRSV">Acts 15:6-35</a></span>).<sup id="cite_ref-Damick2011_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Damick2011-101">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bisschops2017_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bisschops2017-102">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, Judaizing continued to be encouraged for several centuries, particularly by Jewish Christians.<sup id="cite_ref-Damick2011_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Damick2011-101">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Paul opposed the strict applications of Jewish customs for gentile converts,<sup id="cite_ref-Bisschops2017_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bisschops2017-102">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> and argued with the leaders of the Jerusalem Church to allow gentile converts exemption from most <a href="/wiki/613_Mitzvot" class="mw-redirect" title="613 Mitzvot">Jewish commandments</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Jerusalem" title="Council of Jerusalem">Council of Jerusalem</a>, where Paul met with the "pillars of Jerusalem Church" over whether gentile Christians need to keep the Jewish Law and be <a href="/wiki/Circumcision_controversy_in_early_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Circumcision controversy in early Christianity">circumcised</a>. According to Acts, James played a prominent role in the formulation of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Jerusalem" title="Council of Jerusalem">council's</a> decision (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://bible.oremus.org/?passage=Acts+15:19–15:19&amp;version=nrsv">Acts 15:19</a> <a href="/wiki/NRSV" class="mw-redirect" title="NRSV">NRSV</a>) that circumcision was not a requirement. In Galatians, Paul says that James, Peter and <a href="/wiki/John_the_Apostle" title="John the Apostle">John</a><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> will minister to the "circumcised" (in general <a href="/wiki/Jew" class="mw-redirect" title="Jew">Jews</a> and Jewish <a href="/wiki/Proselyte" title="Proselyte">proselytes</a>) in Jerusalem, while Paul and his fellows will minister to the "uncircumcised" (in general gentiles) (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://biblehub.com/galatians/2-9.htm">Galatians 2:9</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;note 13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Catholic_Encyclopedia" title="Catholic Encyclopedia">Catholic Encyclopedia</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> claims: "St. Paul's account of the incident leaves no doubt that St. Peter saw the justice of the rebuke." however, <a href="/wiki/L._Michael_White" title="L. Michael White">L. Michael White</a>'s <i>From Jesus to Christianity</i><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> claims: "The blowup with Peter was a total failure of political bravado, and Paul soon left Antioch as persona non grata, never again to return." Scholar <a href="/wiki/James_D._G._Dunn" class="mw-redirect" title="James D. G. Dunn">James D. G. Dunn</a>, who coined the phrase "<a href="/wiki/New_Perspective_on_Paul" title="New Perspective on Paul">New Perspective on Paul</a>", has proposed that Peter was the "bridge-man" (i.e., the <i>pontifex maximus</i>) between the two other "prominent leading figures" of early Christianity: Paul and James, the brother of Jesus.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenistic_influences">Hellenistic influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Hellenistic influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Talmud</a> scholar <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Boyarin" title="Daniel Boyarin">Daniel Boyarin</a> has argued that Paul's theology of the spirit is more deeply rooted in Hellenistic Judaism than generally believed. In <i>A Radical Jew,</i> Boyarin argues that the Apostle Paul combined the life of Jesus with <a href="/wiki/Greek_philosophy" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek philosophy">Greek philosophy</a> to reinterpret the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a> in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Platonism" title="Platonism">Platonic</a> opposition between the <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_Forms" class="mw-redirect" title="Theory of Forms">ideal</a> (which is real) and the <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">material</a> (which is false). Judaism is a material religion, in which membership is based not on <a href="/wiki/Belief" title="Belief">belief</a> but rather descent from <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a>, physically marked by <a href="/wiki/Religious_male_circumcision" title="Religious male circumcision">circumcision</a>, and focusing on <a href="/wiki/Orthopraxy" title="Orthopraxy">how to live this life properly</a>. Paul saw in the symbol of a resurrected Jesus the possibility of a spiritual rather than corporeal Messiah. He used this notion of Messiah to argue for a religion through which all people — not just descendants of Abraham — could worship the <a href="/wiki/Yahweh" title="Yahweh">God of Abraham</a>. Unlike Judaism, which holds that it is the proper religion only of the Jews, Pauline Christianity claimed to be the proper religion for all people.<sup id="cite_ref-Boyarin_1999_?_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyarin_1999_?-109">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By appealing to the Platonic distinction between the material and the ideal, Paul showed how the spirit of Christ could provide <i>all</i> people a way to worship the God who had previously been worshipped only by Jews, Jewish <a href="/wiki/Proselytes" class="mw-redirect" title="Proselytes">proselytes</a> and <a href="/wiki/God-fearer" title="God-fearer">God-fearers</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> although Jews claimed that he was the <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">one and only God</a> of all. Boyarin roots Paul's work in Hellenistic Judaism and insists that Paul was thoroughly Jewish, but argues that Pauline theology made his version of Christianity appealing to gentiles. Boyarin also sees this Platonic reworking of both Jesus's teachings and Pharisaic Judaism as essential to the emergence of Christianity as a distinct religion, because it justified a Judaism without Jewish law.<sup id="cite_ref-Boyarin_1999_?_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyarin_1999_?-109">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Split_of_early_Christianity_and_Judaism">Split of early Christianity and Judaism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Split of early Christianity and Judaism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergence_as_separate_religious_communities">Emergence as separate religious communities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Emergence as separate religious communities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As Christianity grew throughout the gentile world, the developing Christian tradition diverged from its Jewish and <a href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Jerusalem" title="Early centers of Christianity">Jerusalem roots</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Historians continue to debate the precise moment when early Christianity established itself as a new religion, apart and distinct from Judaism. It is difficult to trace the process by which the two separated or to know exactly when this began. Jewish Christians continued to worship in <a href="/wiki/Synagogue" title="Synagogue">synagogues</a> together with <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple_Judaism" title="Second Temple Judaism">contemporary Jews</a> for centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Some scholars have found evidence of continuous interactions between Jewish-Christian and Rabbinic movements from the mid-to late second century CE to the fourth century CE.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Philip S. Alexander characterizes the question of when Christianity and Judaism parted company and went their separate ways as "one of those deceptively simple questions which should be approached with great care".<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Both <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">Early Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tannaim" title="Tannaim">Early Rabbinical Judaism</a> were far less "orthodox" and less theologically homogeneous than they are today; and both were significantly influenced by <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_religion" title="Hellenistic religion">Hellenistic religion</a> and borrowed allegories and concepts from Classical <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_philosophy" title="Hellenistic philosophy">Hellenistic philosophy</a> and the works of Greek-speaking Jewish authors of the end of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Temple_period" title="Second Temple period">Second Temple period</a> before the two schools of thought eventually firmed up their respective "norms" and doctrines, notably by diverging increasingly on key issues such as the status of "purity laws", the validity of <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Christian" title="Judeo-Christian">Judeo-Christian</a> messianic beliefs, and, more importantly, the use of <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> as <a href="/wiki/Sacerdotal" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacerdotal">sacerdotal</a> languages replacing <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew" title="Biblical Hebrew">Biblical Hebrew</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Daniel_Boyarin_1999_p._15_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daniel_Boyarin_1999_p._15-120">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trajectory">Trajectory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Trajectory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Graetz" title="Heinrich Graetz">Heinrich Graetz</a> postulated a <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Jamnia" title="Council of Jamnia">Council of Jamnia</a> in 90 that excluded Christians from the <a href="/wiki/Synagogues" class="mw-redirect" title="Synagogues">synagogues</a>, but this is disputed. Jewish Christians continued to worship in synagogues for centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to historian <a href="/wiki/Shaye_J._D._Cohen" title="Shaye J. D. Cohen">Shaye J. D. Cohen</a>, "the separation of Christianity from Judaism was a process, not an event", in which the church became "more and more gentile, and less and less Jewish".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen1987228_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen1987228-124">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;note 14&#93;</a></sup> According to Cohen, early Christianity ceased to be a Jewish sect when it ceased to observe Jewish practices, such as circumcision.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen1987168_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen1987168-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> According to Cohen, this process ended in 70 CE, after the great revolt, when various Jewish sects disappeared and Pharisaic Judaism evolved into Rabbinic Judaism, and Christianity emerged as a distinct religion.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Talmudist and professor of Jewish studies <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Boyarin" title="Daniel Boyarin">Daniel Boyarin</a> proposes a revised understanding of the interactions between nascent Christianity and Judaism in late antiquity, viewing the two "new" religions as intensely and complexly intertwined throughout this period. According to Boyarin, Judaism and Christianity "were part of one complex religious family, twins in a womb", for at least three centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-Boyarin_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyarin-127">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;note 15&#93;</a></sup> Alan Segal also states that "one can speak of a 'twin birth' of two new Judaisms, both markedly different from the religious systems that preceded them".<sup id="cite_ref-Segal_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Segal-129">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;note 16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Robert Goldenberg, it is increasingly accepted among scholars that "at the end of the 1st century CE there were not yet two separate religions called 'Judaism' and 'Christianity<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>".<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;note 17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Jewish Christianity fell into decline during the <a href="/wiki/Jewish%E2%80%93Roman_wars" title="Jewish–Roman wars">Jewish–Roman wars</a> (66–135) and the growing <a href="/wiki/Anti-Judaism" title="Anti-Judaism">anti-Judaism</a> perhaps best personified by <a href="/wiki/Marcion_of_Sinope" title="Marcion of Sinope">Marcion of Sinope</a> (c. 150). With persecution by the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Christianity" title="Nicene Christianity">Nicene Christians</a> from the time of the <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I_and_Judaism" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I and Judaism">Roman Emperor Constantine</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_4th_century" title="Christianity in the 4th century">4th century</a>, Jewish Christians sought refuge <a href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Outside_the_Roman_Empire" title="Early centers of Christianity">outside the boundaries of the Empire</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Arabian_Peninsula" title="Early centers of Christianity">Arabia</a> and further afield.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Within the Empire and later elsewhere it was dominated by the gentile based Christianity which became the <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">State church of the Roman Empire</a> and which took control of sites in the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Sepulchre" title="Church of the Holy Sepulchre">Church of the Holy Sepulchre</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cenacle" title="Cenacle">Cenacle</a> and appointed subsequent <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Patriarch_of_Jerusalem" title="Greek Orthodox Patriarch of Jerusalem">Bishops of Jerusalem</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="First_Jewish.E2.80.93Roman_War_and_the_destruction_of_the_Temple"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Jewish–Roman_War_and_the_destruction_of_the_Temple">First Jewish–Roman War and the destruction of the Temple</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: First Jewish–Roman War and the destruction of the Temple">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_Jewish%E2%80%93Roman_War" title="First Jewish–Roman War">First Jewish–Roman War</a></div>
<p>Full scale open revolt against the Romans occurred with the <a href="/wiki/First_Jewish%E2%80%93Roman_War" title="First Jewish–Roman War">First Jewish–Roman War</a> in 66 CE. In 70 CE the Temple was destroyed. The destruction of the Second Temple was a profoundly traumatic experience for the Jews, who were now confronted with difficult and far-reaching questions.<sup id="cite_ref-Neusner_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neusner-134">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;note 18&#93;</a></sup> After the destruction of the Second Temple in 70 CE, sectarianism largely came to an end. The Zealots, Sadducees, and Essenes disappeared, while the <a href="/wiki/Early_Christians" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Christians">Early Christians</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pharisees" title="Pharisees">Pharisees</a> survived, the latter transforming into Rabbinic Judaism, today known simply as "Judaism".
