The term "Pharisee" was no longer used, perhaps because it was a term more often used by non-Pharisees, but also because the term was explicitly sectarian, and the rabbis claimed leadership over all Jews.
</p><p>Many historians argue that the gospels took their final form after the Great Revolt and the destruction of the Temple, although some scholars put the authorship of Mark in the 60s; this could help one understand their context.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Strack theorizes that the growth of a Christian canon (the New Testament) was a factor that influenced the rabbis to record the oral law in writing.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;note 19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A significant contributing factor to the split was the two groups' differing theological interpretations of the Temple's destruction. Rabbinic Judaism saw the destruction as a chastisement for neglecting the Torah. The early Christians however saw it as God’s punishment for the Jewish rejection of Jesus, leading to the claim that the 'true' Israel <a href="/wiki/Supersessionism" title="Supersessionism">was now the Church</a>. Jews believed this claim was scandalous.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> According to Fredriksen, since early Christians believed that Jesus had already replaced the Temple as the expression of a <a href="/wiki/New_Covenant_(theology)" class="mw-redirect" title="New Covenant (theology)">new covenant</a>, they were relatively unconcerned with the destruction of the Temple during the First Jewish-Roman War.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-65">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rejection_of_Jewish_Christianity">Rejection of Jewish Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Rejection of Jewish Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Christian circles, "<a href="/wiki/Nazarene_(sect)" title="Nazarene (sect)">Nazarene</a>" later came to be used as a label for those faithful to Jewish law, in particular for a certain sect. These Jewish Christians, originally the central group in Christianity, were not at first declared to be unorthodox but were later excluded and denounced. Some Jewish Christian groups, such as the <a href="/wiki/Ebionite" class="mw-redirect" title="Ebionite">Ebionites</a>, were considered to have unorthodox beliefs, particularly in relation to their views of Christ and gentile converts. The Nazarenes, holding to orthodoxy except in their adherence to Jewish law, were not deemed heretical until the dominance of <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy" title="Orthodoxy">orthodoxy</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_4th_century" title="Christianity in the 4th century">4th century</a>. The Ebionites may have been a splinter group of Nazarenes, with disagreements over Christology and leadership. After the condemnation of the Nazarenes, "Ebionite" was often used as a general pejorative for all related "heresies".<sup id="cite_ref-Tabor_1998_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tabor_1998-142">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Jewish Christians constituted a separate community from the <a href="/wiki/Pauline_Christianity" title="Pauline Christianity">Pauline Christians</a> but maintained a similar faith, differing only in practice. There was a post-Nicene "double rejection" of the Jewish Christians by both gentile Christianity and Rabbinic Judaism. It is believed that there was no direct confrontation or persecution between gentile and Judaic Christianity. However, by this time the practice of Judeo-Christianity was diluted both by internal schisms and external pressures. Gentile Christianity remained the sole strand of orthodoxy and imposed itself on the previously Jewish Christian sanctuaries, taking full control of those houses of worship by the end of the 5th century.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Growing_anti-Jewish_sentiment_in_Christian_writings">Growing anti-Jewish sentiment in Christian writings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Growing anti-Jewish sentiment in Christian writings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Growing anti-Jewish sentiment among early Christians is evidenced by the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Barnabas" title="Epistle of Barnabas">Epistle of Barnabas</a>, a late-1st/early-2nd century letter attributed to <a href="/wiki/Barnabas" title="Barnabas">Barnabas</a>, the companion of <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul</a> mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts of the Apostles</a>, although it could be by <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Barnabas" title="Epistle of Barnabas">Barnabas of Alexandria</a>, or an anonymous author using the name Barnabas. In no other writing of that early time is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_events_in_early_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="List of events in early Christianity">separation of the gentile Christians from observant Jews</a> so clearly insisted upon. Christians, according to Barnabas, are the only true covenant people, and the Jewish people are no longer in <a href="/wiki/Covenant_(Biblical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Covenant (Biblical)">covenant with God</a>. <a href="/wiki/Circumcision_controversy_in_early_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Circumcision controversy in early Christianity">Circumcision</a> and the entire Jewish sacrificial and ceremonial system have been abolished in favor of "the new law of our Lord Jesus Christ". Barnabas claims that <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Jewish scriptures</a>, rightly understood, serve as a foretelling of Christ and its laws often contain allegorical meanings.
</p><p>While 2nd-century <a href="/wiki/Marcionism" title="Marcionism">Marcionism</a> rejected all Jewish influence on Christianity, <a href="/wiki/Proto-orthodox_Christianity" title="Proto-orthodox Christianity">Proto-orthodox Christianity</a> instead retained some of the doctrines and practices of 1st-century Judaism while rejecting others.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;note 20&#93;</a></sup> They held the <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" class="mw-redirect" title="Tanakh">Jewish scriptures</a> to be authoritative and sacred, employing mostly the <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a> or <a href="/wiki/Targum" title="Targum">Targum</a> translations, and adding other texts as the <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_New_Testament_canon" title="Development of the New Testament canon">New Testament canon developed</a>. Christian <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a> was another continuation of a Judaic practice.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_Jewish_Christianity">Later Jewish Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Later Jewish Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ebionites">Ebionites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Ebionites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ebionites" title="Ebionites">Ebionites</a></div>
<p>The Ebionites were a Jewish Christian movement that existed during the early centuries of the Christian Era.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> They show strong similarities with the earliest form of Jewish Christianity, and their specific theology may have been a "reaction to the <a href="/wiki/Paul_and_Judaism" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul and Judaism">law-free Gentile mission</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2006282_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2006282-148">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> They regarded Jesus as the Messiah while rejecting his <a href="/wiki/Divinity" title="Divinity">divinity</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Virgin_birth_of_Jesus" title="Virgin birth of Jesus">virgin birth</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Ebionites_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ebionites-78">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> and insisted on the necessity of following <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">Jewish law and rites</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> They used the <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_the_Ebionites" title="Gospel of the Ebionites">Gospel of the Ebionites</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Jewish%E2%80%93Christian_gospels" title="Jewish–Christian gospels">Jewish–Christian gospels</a>; the Hebrew Book of Matthew starting at chapter 3; revered <a href="/wiki/James,_brother_of_Jesus" title="James, brother of Jesus">James the brother of Jesus</a> (James the Just); and rejected <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">Paul the Apostle</a> as an <a href="/wiki/Antinomianism#Supporting_Pauline_passages" title="Antinomianism">apostate from the Law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Maccoby_1987_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maccoby_1987-150">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Their name (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">Ἐβιωναῖοι</span> <i>Ebionaioi</i>, derived from <a href="/wiki/Hebrew" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886049626">.mw-parser-output .script-hebrew,.mw-parser-output .script-Hebr{font-size:1.15em;font-family:"Ezra SIL","Ezra SIL SR","Keter Aram Tsova","Taamey Ashkenaz","Taamey David CLM","Taamey Frank CLM","Frank Ruehl CLM","Keter YG","Shofar","David CLM","Hadasim CLM","Simple CLM","Nachlieli","SBL BibLit","SBL Hebrew",Cardo,Alef,"Noto Serif Hebrew","Noto Sans Hebrew","David Libre",David,"Times New Roman",Gisha,Arial,FreeSerif,FreeSans}</style><span class="script-hebrew" style="font-size: 115%;" dir="rtl">אביונים</span> <i>ebyonim</i>, <i>ebionim</i>, meaning "the poor" or "poor ones") suggests that they placed a special value on <a href="/wiki/Vow_of_poverty" class="mw-redirect" title="Vow of poverty">voluntary poverty</a>.
</p><p>Distinctive features of the <i>Gospel of the Ebionites</i> include the absence of the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_birth_of_Jesus" title="Virgin birth of Jesus">virgin birth</a> and of the <a href="/wiki/Genealogy_of_Jesus" title="Genealogy of Jesus">genealogy of Jesus</a>; an <a href="/wiki/Adoptionist" class="mw-redirect" title="Adoptionist">Adoptionist</a> <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christology</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;note 21&#93;</a></sup> in which Jesus is chosen to be <a href="/wiki/Son_of_God" title="Son of God">God's Son</a> at the time of his <a href="/wiki/Baptism_of_Jesus" title="Baptism of Jesus">Baptism</a>; the abolition of the <a href="/wiki/Korban" title="Korban">Jewish sacrifices</a> by Jesus; and an advocacy of <a href="/wiki/Vegetarianism" title="Vegetarianism">vegetarianism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;note 22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nazarenes">Nazarenes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Nazarenes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nazarene_(sect)" title="Nazarene (sect)">Nazarene</a></div>
<p>The Nazarenes originated as a <a href="/wiki/Sect" title="Sect">sect</a> of first-century Judaism. The first use of the term "sect of the Nazarenes" is in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Acts" class="mw-redirect" title="Book of Acts">Book of Acts</a> in the New Testament, where <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul of Tarsus">Paul</a> is accused of being a ringleader of the sect of the Nazarenes ("πρωτοστάτην  τε  τῆς  τῶν  Ναζωραίων  αἱρέσεως").<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;note 23&#93;</a></sup> The term then simply designated followers of "Yeshua Natzri" (<a href="/wiki/Jesus_the_Nazarene" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus the Nazarene">Jesus the Nazarene</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;note 24&#93;</a></sup> but in the first to fourth centuries the term was used for a sect of followers of Jesus who were closer to Judaism than most Christians.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> They are described by <a href="/wiki/Epiphanius_of_Salamis" title="Epiphanius of Salamis">Epiphanius of Salamis</a> and are mentioned later by <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> who made a distinction between the Nazarenes of their time and the "Nazarenes" mentioned in Acts 24:5.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;note 25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Nazarenes were similar to the <a href="/wiki/Ebionites" title="Ebionites">Ebionites</a>, in that they considered themselves <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>, maintained an adherence to the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Moses" title="Law of Moses">Law of Moses</a>, and used only the <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_the_Hebrews" title="Gospel of the Hebrews">Gospel of the Hebrews</a></i>, rejecting all the <a href="/wiki/Canonical_gospels" class="mw-redirect" title="Canonical gospels">Canonical gospels</a>.  However, unlike half of the Ebionites, they accepted the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_birth_of_Jesus" title="Virgin birth of Jesus">Virgin Birth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Krauss_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krauss-159">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hegg_2007_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hegg_2007-160">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Gospel of the Hebrews</i> was a <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretic</a> <a href="/wiki/Jewish%E2%80%93Christian_gospels" title="Jewish–Christian gospels">Jewish–Christian gospel</a>, the text of which is <a href="/wiki/Lost_work" title="Lost work">lost</a>; only fragments of it survive as brief quotations by the early <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a> and in apocryphal writings.  The fragments contain traditions of <a href="/wiki/Pre-existence_of_Christ" title="Pre-existence of Christ">Jesus' pre-existence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Incarnation" title="Incarnation">incarnation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baptism_of_Jesus" title="Baptism of Jesus">baptism</a>, and probable <a href="/wiki/Temptation_of_Christ" title="Temptation of Christ">temptation</a>, along with some of his <a href="/wiki/Sayings_of_Jesus" class="mw-redirect" title="Sayings of Jesus">sayings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECameron1992105–6_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECameron1992105–6-161">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Distinctive features include a <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">Christology</a> characterized by the belief that the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a> is Jesus' <a href="/wiki/Shekhinah" title="Shekhinah">Divine Mother</a>; and a first <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_appearances_of_Jesus" class="mw-redirect" title="Resurrection appearances of Jesus">resurrection appearance</a> to <a href="/wiki/James_the_Just" class="mw-redirect" title="James the Just">James, the brother of Jesus</a>, showing a high regard for James as the leader of the <a href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Jerusalem" title="Early centers of Christianity">Jewish Christian church in Jerusalem</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoch1990364_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoch1990364-162">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> It was probably composed in <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Greek</a> in the first decades of the 2nd century, and is believed to have been used by Greek-speaking Jewish Christians in <a href="/wiki/Early_centers_of_Christianity#Alexandria" title="Early centers of Christianity">Egypt</a> during that century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELapham2003159,163_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELapham2003159,163-163">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_the_Nazarenes" title="Gospel of the Nazarenes">Gospel of the Nazarenes</a> is the title given to fragments of one of the lost <a href="/wiki/Jewish-Christian_Gospels" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish-Christian Gospels">Jewish-Christian Gospels</a> of Matthew partially reconstructed from the writings of <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Surviving_Byzantine_and_.27Syriac.27_communities_in_the_Middle_East"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Surviving_Byzantine_and_'Syriac'_communities_in_the_Middle_East">Surviving Byzantine and 'Syriac' communities in the Middle East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Surviving Byzantine and &#039;Syriac&#039; communities in the Middle East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some typically <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Grecian</a> "Ancient <a href="/wiki/Synagogal" class="mw-redirect" title="Synagogal">Synagogal</a>" <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">priestly</a> <a href="/wiki/Rite" title="Rite">rites</a> have survived partially to the present, notably in the distinct <a href="/wiki/Church_service" title="Church service">church service</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church_of_Antioch" title="Greek Orthodox Church of Antioch">Greek Orthodox Church of Antioch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syriac Orthodox Church</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Melkite" title="Melkite">Melkite</a> <a href="/wiki/Greek_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Catholic">Greek Catholic</a> communities of the <a href="/wiki/Hatay_Province" title="Hatay Province">Hatay Province</a> of Southern Turkey, Syria and Lebanon.
</p><p>The unique combination of <a href="/wiki/Ethnocultural" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnocultural">ethnocultural</a> traits inhered from the fusion of a <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a>-<a href="/wiki/Ancient_Macedonians" title="Ancient Macedonians">Macedonian</a> cultural base, <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Judaism" title="Hellenistic Judaism">Hellenistic Judaism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> civilization gave birth to the distinctly Antiochian "Middle Eastern-Roman" Christian traditions of Cilicia (Southeastern Turkey) and Syria/Lebanon:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The mixture of Roman, Greek, and Jewish elements admirably adapted Antioch for the great part it played in the early history of Christianity. The city was the cradle of the church.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Members of these communities still call themselves <a href="/wiki/R%C3%BBm" title="Rûm">Rûm</a> which literally means "<a href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Roman">Eastern Roman</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Byzantine" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a>" or "Asian Greek" in <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>. The term "Rûm" is used in preference to <i>"Ionani"</i> or <i>"Yāvāni"</i> which means "European Greek" or "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Ionian" class="extiw" title="wikt:Ionian">Ionian</a>" in <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Classical Arabic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew" title="Biblical Hebrew">Ancient Hebrew</a>.
</p><p>Most <a href="/wiki/Middle-East" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle-East">Middle-Eastern</a> "<a href="/wiki/Melkite" title="Melkite">Melkites</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/R%C3%BBm" title="Rûm">Rûms</a>", can trace their <a href="/wiki/Ethnocultural" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnocultural">ethnocultural</a> heritage to the Southern Anatolian ('Cilician') and Syrian Hellenized Greek-speaking <a href="/wiki/Jewish" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish">Jewish</a> communities of the past and <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macedonians_(Greeks)" title="Macedonians (Greeks)">Macedonian</a> <a href="/wiki/Settler" title="Settler">settlers</a> ('Greco-Syrians'), founders of the original "Antiochian Greek" communities of <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a>, Northwestern Syria and Lebanon. Counting members of the surviving minorities in the <a href="/wiki/Hatay_Province" title="Hatay Province">Hatay Province</a> of Turkey, in Syria, Lebanon, Northern Israel and their relatives in the diaspora, there are more than 1.8 million Greco-Melkite Christians residing in the Northern-MENA, the US, Canada and Latin America today i.e. <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Orthodox">Greek Orthodox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Catholic">Greek Catholic</a> Christians under the ancient <a href="/wiki/Jurisdictional" class="mw-redirect" title="Jurisdictional">jurisdictional</a> authority of the <a href="/wiki/Patriarchate" title="Patriarchate">patriarchates</a> of Antioch and Jerusalem ("Orthodox" in the narrow sense) or their <a href="/wiki/Uniat" class="mw-redirect" title="Uniat">Uniat</a> offshoots ("Catholic" or "united" with Rome).
</p><p>Today, certain families are associated with descent from the early Jewish Christians of Antioch, Damascus, <a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">Judea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Galilee" title="Galilee">Galilee</a>. Some of those families carry surnames such as <a href="/wiki/John_(surname)" title="John (surname)">Youhanna</a> (John), <a href="/wiki/Hananiah_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hananiah (disambiguation)">Hanania</a> (Ananias), <a href="/wiki/Zion" title="Zion">Sahyoun</a> (Zion), <a href="/wiki/Elijah" title="Elijah">Eliyya/Elias</a> (Elijah), <a href="/wiki/Chamoun" title="Chamoun">Chamoun/Shamoun</a> (Simeon/Simon), <a href="/wiki/Semaan" title="Semaan">Semaan/Simaan</a> (Simeon/Simon), <a href="/wiki/Manasseh" class="mw-redirect" title="Manasseh">Menassa</a> (Manasseh), <a href="/wiki/Solomon" title="Solomon">Salamoun</a>/<a href="/wiki/Suleiman" title="Suleiman">Suleiman</a> (Solomon), <a href="/wiki/Joachim" title="Joachim">Youwakim</a> (Joachim), <a href="/wiki/Zacharias_(surname)" title="Zacharias (surname)">Zakariya</a> (Zacharias) and others.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_movements">Contemporary movements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Contemporary movements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In modern days, the term "Jewish Christian" generally refers to ethnic Jews who have converted to or have been raised in Christianity. They are mostly members of <a href="/wiki/Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic">Catholic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox Christian</a> congregations, and are generally <a href="/wiki/Jewish_assimilation" title="Jewish assimilation">assimilated</a> into the Christian mainstream, although they retain a strong sense of their <a href="/wiki/Jewish_identity" title="Jewish identity">Jewish identity</a>. Some Jewish Christians also refer to themselves as "<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Christian_movement" title="Hebrew Christian movement">Hebrew Christians</a>".
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Christian_movement" title="Hebrew Christian movement">Hebrew Christian movement</a> of the 19th century was a largely <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican Church">Anglican</a>-led and largely integrated initiative, led by figures such as <a href="/wiki/Michael_Solomon_Alexander" class="mw-redirect" title="Michael Solomon Alexander">Michael Solomon Alexander</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglican-German_Bishopric_in_Jerusalem#The_bishopric_in_practice" title="Anglican-German Bishopric in Jerusalem">Bishop of Jerusalem</a> 1842–1845; some figures, such as <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Frey" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph Frey">Joseph Frey</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/London_Society_for_Promoting_Christianity_amongst_the_Jews" class="mw-redirect" title="London Society for Promoting Christianity amongst the Jews">London Society for Promoting Christianity amongst the Jews</a>, were more assertive of Jewish identity and independence.
</p><p>The 19th century saw at least 250,000 Jews convert to Christianity according to existing records of various societies.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Data from the <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> has it that, as of 2013, about 1.6 million adult <a href="/wiki/American_Jews" title="American Jews">American Jews</a> identify themselves as <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a>, most as <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pew:_portrait_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pew:_portrait-168">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> According to the same data, most of the Jews who identify themselves as some sort of Christian (1.6 million) were raised as Jews or are Jews by ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-pew:_portrait_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pew:_portrait-168">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> According to a 2012 study, 17% of <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Russia" title="History of the Jews in Russia">Jews in Russia</a> identify themselves as <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Russia" title="Christianity in Russia">Christians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ArenaAtlas_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ArenaAtlas-170">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2012maps_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2012maps-171">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Messianic_Judaism" title="Messianic Judaism">Messianic Judaism</a> is a religious movement that incorporates elements of Judaism with the <a href="/wiki/Christianity#Beliefs" title="Christianity">tenets of Christianity</a>. Adherents, many of whom are ethnically Jewish, worship in congregations that include <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_prayers" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebrew prayers">Hebrew prayers</a>. They baptize messianic believers who are of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_accountability#Judaism" class="mw-redirect" title="Age of accountability">age of accountability</a> (able to accept Jesus as the Messiah), often observe kosher
<a href="/wiki/Kashrut" title="Kashrut">dietary laws</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shabbat" title="Shabbat">Saturday as the Sabbath</a>. Although they do recognize the Christian <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> as holy scripture, most do not use the label "Christian" to describe themselves.
</p><p>The two groups are not completely distinct; some adherents, for example, favor Messianic congregations but freely live in both worlds, such as theologian <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Fruchtenbaum" title="Arnold Fruchtenbaum">Arnold Fruchtenbaum</a>, the founder of Ariel Ministries.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">
<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">
<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:P_christianity.svg" class="image"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/31px-P_christianity.svg.png" decoding="async" width="31" height="28" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/47px-P_christianity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/P_christianity.svg/62px-P_christianity.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="360" /></a></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Christianity" title="Portal:Christianity">Christianity portal</a></span></li>
<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_of_David.svg/24px-Star_of_David.svg.png" decoding="async" width="24" height="28" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_of_David.svg/36px-Star_of_David.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_of_David.svg/48px-Star_of_David.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="260" data-file-height="300" /></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Judaism" title="Portal:Judaism">Judaism portal</a></span></li>
<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/P_history.svg/31px-P_history.svg.png" decoding="async" width="31" height="28" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/P_history.svg/47px-P_history.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/P_history.svg/62px-P_history.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="360" /></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:History" title="Portal:History">History portal</a></span></li>
<li style="display:table-row"><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/SPQRomani.svg/32px-SPQRomani.svg.png" decoding="async" width="32" height="19" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/SPQRomani.svg/48px-SPQRomani.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/SPQRomani.svg/64px-SPQRomani.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="931" data-file-height="548" /></span><span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Ancient_Rome" title="Portal:Ancient Rome">Ancient Rome portal</a></span></li></ul></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Christian_movement" title="Hebrew Christian movement">Hebrew Christian movement</a> – a 19th-century movement of Jewish converts to Christianity acting semi-autonomously within the Anglican and other established churches.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> though it is also used in some texts concerning the early church,<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> and Arnold Fruchtenbaum applied the term to Jewish Christians standing aside from the <a href="/wiki/Messianic_Judaism" title="Messianic Judaism">Messianic Judaism</a> movement.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Roots" title="Hebrew Roots">Hebrew Roots</a> – A religious movement that embraces both Old and New Testaments but without the observance of the Jewish Talmud and many Jewish traditions not supported by Scripture.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Judaizers" title="Judaizers">Judaizers</a> – <a href="/wiki/Early_Christian" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Christian">Early Christians</a> who maintained or adopted Jewish religious practices, from the period of the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_1st_century" title="Christianity in the 1st century">inception of Christianity</a> until approximately the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_5th_century" title="Christianity in the 5th century">fifth century</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jewish_Christian&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It appears in the Acts of the Apostles, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Acts%209:2–9:2&amp;version=nkjv">Acts 9:2</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Acts%2019:9–19:9&amp;version=nkjv">Acts 19:9</a> and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Acts%2019:23–19:23&amp;version=nkjv">Acts 19:23</a>). Some <a href="/wiki/English_translations_of_the_bible" class="mw-redirect" title="English translations of the bible">English translations of the New Testament</a> capitalize 'the Way' (e.g. the <a href="/wiki/New_King_James_Version" title="New King James Version">New King James Version</a> and the <a href="/wiki/English_Standard_Version" title="English Standard Version">English Standard Version</a>), indicating that this was how 'the new religion seemed then to be designated' <sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> whereas others treat the phrase as indicative—'the way',<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> 'that way' <sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> or 'the way of the Lord'.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a> version reads, "the way of God" and the <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate Latin</a> version, "the way of the Lord".<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><br />See also <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://biblethingsinbibleways.wordpress.com/2013/11/21/sect-of-the-way-the-nazarenes-christians-names-given-to-the-early-church/"><i>Sect of “The Way”, “The Nazarenes” &amp; “Christians”&#160;: Names given to the Early Church</i></a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFEddyBoyd2007">Eddy &amp; Boyd (2007)</a>, p.&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=WgROZMp4zDMC&amp;pg=PA136">136</a>: "Burton Mack argues that Paul’s view of Jesus as a divine figure who gives his life for the salvation of others had to originate in a Hellenistic rather than a Jewish environment. Mack writes, "Such a notion [of vicarious human suffering] cannot be traced to old Jewish and/ or Israelite traditions, for the very notion of a vicarious human sacrifice was anathema in these cultures. But it can be traced to a Strong Greek tradition of extolling a noble death." More specifically, Mack argues that a Greek "myth of martyrdom" and the "noble death" tradition are ultimately responsible for influencing the hellenized Jews of the Christ cults to develop a divinized Jesus."<br /> <a href="#CITEREFEddyBoyd2007">Eddy &amp; Boyd (2007)</a>, p.&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=WgROZMp4zDMC&amp;pg=PA93">93</a>further note that "The most sophisticated and influential version of the hellenization thesis was forged within the German <i>Religionsgeschichtliche Schule</i> of the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries—now often referred to as the “old history of religions school.” Here, the crowning literary achievement in several ways is Wilhelm Bousset’s 1913 work <i>Kyrios Christos</i>. Bousset envisions two forms of pre-Pauline Christianity: [1. In the early Palestinian community, and 2. In the Hellenistic communities.]"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See for comparison: <i><a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophet</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/False_prophet" title="False prophet">false prophet</a></i>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The notion of Apocalyptic prophet is shared by <a href="/wiki/E._P._Sanders" title="E. P. Sanders">E. P. Sanders</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> a main proponent of the <a href="/wiki/New_Perspective_on_Paul" title="New Perspective on Paul">New Perspective on Paul</a>, and Bart Ehrman.<sup id="cite_ref-Ehrman.1april2018_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrman.1april2018-37">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bouma_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bouma-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ehrman: "What started Christianity was the Belief in the Resurrection.  It was nothing else.  Followers of Jesus came to believe he had been raised.  They did not believe it because of “proof” such as the empty tomb.  They believed it because some of them said they saw Jesus alive afterward.  Others who believed these stories told others who also came to believe them.  These others told others who told others – for days, weeks, months, years, decades, centuries, and now millennia.  Christianity is all about believing what others have said.  It has always been that way and always will be.<br /><br />Easter is the celebration of the first proclamation that Jesus did not remain dead.  It is not that his body was resuscitated after a Near Death Experience.   God had exalted Jesus to heaven never to die again; he will (soon) return from heaven to rule the earth.  This is a statement of faith, not a matter of empirical proof.  Christians themselves believe it.  Non-Christians recognize it as the very heart of the Christian message.  It is a message based on faith in what other people claimed and testified based on what others claimed and testified based on what others claimed and testified – all the way back to the first followers of Jesus who said they saw Jesus alive afterward.<sup id="cite_ref-Ehrman.1april2018_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrman.1april2018-37">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-74"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-74">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Porter, Hayes and Tombs: "Stanley Porter's paper brings together a body of literature, hitherto largely neglected, which highlights the fact that the Greeks, contrary to much scholarly opinion, did have a significant tradition of bodily resurrection, and that the Jewish tradition emphasizes a continued spiritual existence rather than a bodily resurrection. Thus, Paul in the New Testament probably adopted Graeco-Roman assumptions regarding the resurrection, although he was not blindly derivative in developing his conceptual framework."<sup id="cite_ref-Porter.Hayes.Tombs_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porter.Hayes.Tombs-71">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-81">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Several authors have even argued for influences from a "pre-Christian Gnostic redeemer myth". According to Dunn, this interpretation is dated, and based on "a most questionable historical foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2006146-147_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2006146-147-80">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-82">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Several authors have even argued for influences from a "pre-Christian Gnostic redeemer myth". According to Dunn, this interpretation is dated, and based on "a most questionable historical foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2006146-147_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2006146-147-80">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> while others have argued for Jewish influences. According to Dunn, the hymn contains a contrast with the sins of Adam and his disobedience. Dunn further notes that the hymn may be seen as a three-stage Christology, starting with "an earlier stage of mythic pre-history or pre-existence," but regards the humility-exaltation contrast to be the main theme.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2006146-147_80-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2006146-147-80">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-86">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The term "Pauline Christianity" is generally considered a <a href="/wiki/Pejorative" title="Pejorative">pejorative</a> by mainstream Christianity, as it carries the implication that Christianity is a corruption of the original teachings of Jesus, as for example in the belief of a <a href="/wiki/Great_Apostasy" title="Great Apostasy">Great Apostasy</a> as found in <a href="/wiki/Restorationism_(Christian_primitivism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Restorationism (Christian primitivism)">Restorationism</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Most of <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy" title="Orthodoxy">orthodox</a> Christianity relies heavily on these teachings and considers them to be amplifications and explanations of the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Jesus" title="Ministry of Jesus">teachings of Jesus</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-88"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-88">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to Mack, he may have been converted to another early strand of Christianity, with a High Christology.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMack1997109_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMack1997109-87">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-90"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-90">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to Mack,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMack198898_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMack198898-89">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> "Paul was converted to a Hellenized form of some Jesus movement that had already developed into a Christ cult. [...] Thus his letters serve as documentation for the Christ cult as well." <a href="#CITEREFPrice2000">Price (2000)</a>, p.&#160;75, §.&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=VJh1H-hf5EwC&amp;pg=PA75">The Christ Cults</a> comments: "By choosing the terminology “Christ cults,” Burton Mack means to differentiate those early movements that revered Jesus as the Christ from those that did not. [...] Mack is perhaps not quite clear about what would constitute a Christ cult. Or at least he seems to me to obscure some important distinctions between what would appear to be significantly different subtypes of Christ movements."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-97"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-97">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Galatians 1:13.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2006294_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2006294-93">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> According to Dunn, Paul persecuted the "Hellenists"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2006294_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2006294-93">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> of Acts 6.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunn2006289_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunn2006289-94">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> According to Larry Hurtado, there was no theological divide between "Hellenists" (greek speaking Jews from the diaspora who had returned to Jerusalem) and their fellow Jesus-followers; Paul's persecution was directed against the Jesus-movement in general, because it offended his Pharisaic convictions.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-105"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-105">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">These terms (circumcised/uncircumcised) are generally interpreted to mean <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a>, who were predominant; however, this is an oversimplification, as 1st-century <a href="/wiki/Judaea_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="Judaea Province">Judaea Province</a> also had some Jews who no longer circumcised and some Greeks and others such as Egyptians, Ethiopians, and Arabs who did.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-125"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-125">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Cohen: "The separation of Christianity from Judaism was a process, not an event. The essential part of this process was that the church was becoming more and more gentile, and less and less Jewish, but the separation manifested itself in different ways in each local community where Jews and Christians dwelt together. In some places, the Jews expelled the Christians; in other, the Christians left of their own accord."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen1987228_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen1987228-124">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-128"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-128">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Boyarin: "for at least the first three centuries of their common lives, Judaism in all of its forms and Christianity in all of its forms were part of one complex religious family, twins in a womb, contending with each other for identity and precedence, but sharing with each other the same spiritual food."<sup id="cite_ref-Boyarin_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boyarin-127">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-130"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-130">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Segal: "one can speak of a 'twin birth' of two new Judaisms, both markedly different from the religious systems that preceded them. Not only were Rabbinic Judaism and Christianity religious twins, but, like Jacob and Esau, the twin sons of Isaac and Rebecca, they fought in the womb, setting the stage for life after the womb."<sup id="cite_ref-Segal_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Segal-129">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-132"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-132">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Boyarin adds that "Without the power of the <a href="/wiki/Proto-orthodox_Christianity" title="Proto-orthodox Christianity">orthodox Church</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rabbi" title="Rabbi">rabbis</a> to declare people <a href="/wiki/Heretics" class="mw-redirect" title="Heretics">heretics</a> and outside the system it remained impossible to declare phenomenologically who was a Jew and who was a Christian. At least as interesting and significant, it seems more and more clear that it is frequently impossible to tell a Jewish text from a Christian text. The borders are fuzzy, and this has consequences. Religious ideas and innovations can cross borders in both directions.<sup id="cite_ref-Daniel_Boyarin_1999_p._15_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daniel_Boyarin_1999_p._15-120">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-135"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-135">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Such as:<sup id="cite_ref-Neusner_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neusner-134">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
<ul><li>How to achieve atonement without the Temple?</li>
<li>How to explain the disastrous outcome of the rebellion?</li>
<li>How to live in the post-Temple, Romanized world?</li>
<li>How to connect present and past traditions?</li></ul>
How people answered these questioned depended largely on their position prior to the revolt.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-140"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-140">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The theory that the destruction of the Temple and subsequent upheaval led to the committing of Oral Law into writing was first explained in the Epistle of <a href="/wiki/Sherira_Gaon" title="Sherira Gaon">Sherira Gaon</a> and often repeated. See, for example, Grayzel, <i>A History of the Jews</i>, Penguin Books, 1984, p. 193.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-145"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-145">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_law_in_Christianity#Historical_background" class="mw-redirect" title="Biblical law in Christianity">Historical background to the issue of Biblical law in Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">Early Christianity</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-151"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-151">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFKloppenborg1994">Kloppenborg 1994</a>, pp.&#160;435–9; p.&#160;435&#160;– "This belief, known as "adoptionism", held that Jesus was not divine by nature or by birth, but that God chose him to become his son, i.e., adopted him."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-152"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-152">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFVielhauerStrecker1991">Vielhauer &amp; Strecker 1991</a>, pp.&#160;166–71; p.&#160;168&#160;– "Jesus' task is to do away with the 'sacrifices'. In this saying (16.4–5), the hostility of the Ebionites against the Temple cult is documented."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-153"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-153">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Acts 24:5 "For we have found this man a pestilent fellow, and a mover of sedition among all the Jews throughout the world, and a ringleader of the sect of the Nazarenes."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-154"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-154">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> term <span lang="he" dir="rtl" title="Hebrew language text">נוֹצְרִי</span> (<i><i lang="he-Latn" title="Hebrew-language romanization">nôṣrî</i></i>) still does</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-158"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-158">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Edward Hare <i>The principal doctrines of Christianity defended</i> 1837 p318: "The Nazarenes of ecclesiastical history adhered to the law of their fathers; whereas when Tertullus accused Paul as 'a ringleader of the sect of the Nazarenes', he accused him as one who despised the law, and 'had gone about to the temple', Acts xxiv, 5, 6. "</span>
</li>
</ol></div>

<li id="cite_note-JVL-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-JVL_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-JVL_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-JVL_2-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-JVL_2-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-JVL_2-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-JVL_2-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-JVL_2-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jewishvirtuallibrary.org/christianity-2">"Christianity: Severance from Judaism"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Jewish_Virtual_Library" title="Jewish Virtual Library">Jewish Virtual Library</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/American%E2%80%93Israeli_Cooperative_Enterprise" title="American–Israeli Cooperative Enterprise">AICE</a>. 2008<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">17 December</span> 2018</span>. <q>A major difficulty in tracing the growth of Christianity from its beginnings as a <a href="/wiki/Jewish_messianism" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish messianism">Jewish messianic sect</a>, and its relations to the various other normative-Jewish, sectarian-Jewish, and Christian-Jewish groups is presented by the fact that what ultimately became normative Christianity was originally but one among various contending Christian trends. Once the "gentile Christian" trend won out, and the <a href="/wiki/Pauline_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Pauline theology">teaching</a> of <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul of Tarsus">Paul</a> became accepted as expressing the doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">Church</a>, the Jewish Christian groups were pushed to the margin and ultimately excluded as heretical. Being rejected both by normative Judaism and the Church, they ultimately disappeared. Nevertheless, several Jewish Christian sects (such as the <a href="/wiki/Nazarene_(sect)" title="Nazarene (sect)">Nazarenes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ebionites" title="Ebionites">Ebionites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elchasaites" class="mw-redirect" title="Elchasaites">Elchasaites</a>, and others) existed for some time, and a few of them seem to have endured for several centuries. Some sects saw in Jesus mainly a <a href="/wiki/Prophet#Judaism" title="Prophet">prophet</a> and not the "Christ," others seem to have believed in him as the Messiah, but did not draw the <a href="/wiki/Christology" title="Christology">christological</a> and other conclusions that subsequently became fundamental in the teaching of the Church (the divinity of the Christ, <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">trinitarian conception of the Godhead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abrogation_of_Old_Covenant_laws" title="Abrogation of Old Covenant laws">abrogation of the Law</a>). After the disappearance of the early Jewish Christian sects and the triumph of gentile Christianity, to become a Christian meant, for a Jew, to <a href="/wiki/Apostasy_in_Judaism" title="Apostasy in Judaism">apostatize</a> and to leave the Jewish community.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Jewish+Virtual+Library&amp;rft.atitle=Christianity%3A+Severance+from+Judaism&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.jewishvirtuallibrary.org%2Fchristianity-2&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJewish+Christian" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-Judaeo-Christians-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Judaeo-Christians_13-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Judaeo-Christians_13-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Judaeo-Christians_13-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Judaeo-Christians_13-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Tomson, Peter J.; Lambers-Petry, Doris, eds. (2003). <i>The Image of the Judaeo-Christians in Ancient Jewish and Christian Literature</i>. Wissenschaftliche Untersuchungen zum Neuen Testament. <b>158</b>. <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BCbingen" title="Tübingen">Tübingen</a>: <a href="/wiki/Mohr_Siebeck" title="Mohr Siebeck">Mohr Siebeck</a>. p.&#160;162. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/3-16-148094-5" title="Special:BookSources/3-16-148094-5"><bdi>3-16-148094-5</bdi></a>. <q>Though every definition of <i>Jewish Christians</i> has problems, the most useful is probably that they were believers in Jesus, of ethnic Jewish origin, who <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">observed the Torah</a> and so retained their <a href="/wiki/Jewish_identity" title="Jewish identity">Jewish identity</a>.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Image+of+the+Judaeo-Christians+in+Ancient+Jewish+and+Christian+Literature&amp;rft.place=T%C3%BCbingen&amp;rft.series=Wissenschaftliche+Untersuchungen+zum+Neuen+Testament&amp;rft.pages=162&amp;rft.pub=Mohr+Siebeck&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.isbn=3-16-148094-5&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJewish+Christian" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Tabor-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Tabor_14-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Tabor_14-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/James_D._Tabor" class="mw-redirect" title="James D. Tabor">Tabor, James D.</a> (2013). <i>Paul and Jesus: How the Apostle Transformed Christianity</i>. <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a>: <a href="/wiki/Simon_%26_Schuster" title="Simon &amp; Schuster">Simon &amp; Schuster</a>. p.&#160;24. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4391-3498-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-4391-3498-6"><bdi>978-1-4391-3498-6</bdi></a>. <q>[...] the original apostolic Christianity that came before Paul, and developed independently of him, by those who had known and spent time with Jesus, was in sharp contrast to <a href="/wiki/Pauline_Christianity" title="Pauline Christianity">Paul's version of the new faith</a>. This lost Christianity held sway during Paul's lifetime, and only with the <a href="/wiki/James,_brother_of_Jesus" title="James, brother of Jesus">death of James</a> in A.D. 62, followed by the brutal <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(70_CE)" title="Siege of Jerusalem (70 CE)">destruction of Jerusalem by the Romans in A.D. 70</a>, did it begin to lose its influence as the center of Jesus movement. Ironically, it was the <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_New_Testament_canon" title="Development of the New Testament canon">production and final editing of the New Testament itself</a> [...] supporting Paul's version of Christianity, that ensured first the marginalization, and subsequently the death of this original form of Christianity.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Paul+and+Jesus%3A+How+the+Apostle+Transformed+Christianity&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pages=24&amp;rft.pub=Simon+%26+Schuster&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-4391-3498-6&amp;rft.aulast=Tabor&amp;rft.aufirst=James+D.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJewish+Christian" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Theological dictionary of the New Testament</i> (1972), p. 568. Gerhard Kittel, Geoffrey William Bromiley, Gerhard Friedrich: "When the Jewish Christians whom James sent from Jerusalem arrived at Antioch, Cephas withdrew from table-fellowship with the Gentile Christians".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Cynthia White, <i>The emergence of Christianity</i> (2007), p. 36: "In these early days of the church in Jerusalem there was a growing antagonism between the Greek-speaking Hellenized Jewish Christians and the Aramaic-speaking Jewish Christians".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Michele Murray, <i>Playing a Jewish game: Gentile Christian Judaizing in the first and Second Centuries CE</i>, Canadian Corporation for Studies in Religion (2004), p. 97: "Justin is obviously frustrated by continued law observance by Gentile Christians; to impede the spread of the phenomenon, he declares that he does not approve of Jewish Christians who attempt to influence Gentile Christians".</span>

<li id="cite_note-106"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-106">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.newadvent.org/cathen/08537a.htm">"CATHOLIC ENCYCLOPEDIA: Judaizers"</a>. <i>www.newadvent.org</i>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=www.newadvent.org&amp;rft.atitle=CATHOLIC+ENCYCLOPEDIA%3A+Judaizers&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.newadvent.org%2Fcathen%2F08537a.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJewish+Christian" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-107"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-107">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">L. Michael White (2004). <i>From Jesus to Christianity</i>. Harper San Francisco. p.&#160;170. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-06-052655-6" title="Special:BookSources/0-06-052655-6"><bdi>0-06-052655-6</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=From+Jesus+to+Christianity&amp;rft.pages=170&amp;rft.pub=Harper+San+Francisco&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.isbn=0-06-052655-6&amp;rft.au=L.+Michael+White&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJewish+Christian" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-108"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-108">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>The Canon Debate</i>, McDonald &amp; Sanders editors, 2002, chapter 32, page 577, by James D. G. Dunn: "For <i>Peter was probably in fact and effect the bridge-man</i> (pontifex maximus!) <i>who did more than any other to hold together the diversity of first-century Christianity.</i> James the brother of Jesus and Paul, the two other most prominent leading figures in first-century Christianity, were too much identified with their respective "brands" of Christianity, at least in the eyes of Christians at the opposite ends of this particular spectrum. But Peter, as shown particularly by the Antioch episode in Gal 2, had both a care to hold firm to his Jewish heritage, which Paul lacked, and an openness to the demands of developing Christianity, which James lacked. John might have served as such a figure of the center holding together the extremes, but if the writings linked with his name are at all indicative of his own stance he was too much of an individualist to provide such a rallying point. Others could link the developing new religion more firmly to its founding events and to Jesus himself. But none of them, including the rest of the twelve, seem to have played any role of continuing significance for the whole sweep of Christianity—though James the brother of John might have proved an exception had he been spared." [Italics original]</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-146"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-146">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://jewishencyclopedia.com/view.jsp?artid=222&amp;letter=B&amp;search=Baptism">Jewish Encyclopedia: Baptism</a>: "According to rabbinical teachings, which dominated even during the existence of the Temple (Pes. viii. 8), Baptism, next to circumcision and sacrifice, was an absolutely necessary condition to be fulfilled by a <a href="/wiki/Proselyte" title="Proselyte">proselyte</a> to Judaism (Yeb. 46b, 47b; Ker. 9a; 'Ab. Zarah 57a; Shab. 135a; Yer. Kid. iii. 14, 64d). Circumcision, however, was much more important, and, like baptism, was called a "seal" (Schlatter, <i>Die Kirche Jerusalems</i>, 1898, p. 70).</span>
